{"title": "Cleveland-Cliffs stock drops on $80 million Q1 energy cost", "link": "https://sg.finance.yahoo.com/news/cleveland-cliffs-stock-drops-on-80-million-q1-energy-cost-164014830.html", "text": "What happened: Cleveland-Cliffs (CLF) stock fell as much as 11% on Monday before paring losses to 3%.\n\nWhat\u2019s moving the stock: Shares fell after the company disclosed an unexpected $80 million energy cost in the first quarter, driven by the extreme cold snap that sent arctic temperatures sweeping across the US.\n\nCEO Lourenco Goncalves said the company typically locks in natural gas prices three days before each month begins. For February, that date happened to coincide with the peak of January pricing, Goncalves said.\n\nEven as gas prices have fallen since January, that drop has been partially offset by increased expenses elsewhere, Goncalves said. He cited the rising cost of fuel, which has jumped as the war in Iran has snarled global energy markets.\n\nWhat else you should know: Goncalves also said Monday that due to a run-up in steel prices and healthy demand from the US automotive sector, Cleveland-Cliffs is \u201cno longer in a hurry\u201d to close a long-planned deal with South Korea\u2019s POSCO Holdings (PKX).\n\n\u201cOur situation is getting better, and that\u2019s changing our perception of how this deal should be taken care of,\u201d Goncalves said on Monday. \u201cWe are no longer in a hurry.\u201d\n\nThe deal was expected to be finalized in the fourth quarter of 2025 or the first quarter of 2026.\n\nHigher energy costs and a delayed deal overshadowed a smaller-than-expected profit loss in the first quarter. Cleveland-Cliffs reported a loss per share of $0.42, compared to Wall Street estimates for a loss of $0.44 per share, according to S&P Global Market Intelligence. Revenue of $4.92 billion surpassed expectations for $4.79 billion.\n\nClick here for the latest stock market news and in-depth analysis, including events that move stocks"}

{"title": "Home sellers outnumber homebuyers in low-demand market", "link": "https://sg.finance.yahoo.com/news/home-sellers-outnumber-homebuyers-in-low-demand-market-162240040.html", "text": "So far, homebuyers aren\u2019t lining up this spring.\n\nRedfin estimates there were 1.39 million homebuyers in the market last month, not far off the recent low of 1.38 million seen at the onset of the pandemic in April 2020.\n\nThe recent figures show a 10% drop compared to March 2025. Meanwhile, roughly 1.99 million people are looking to sell, up slightly from a year ago but down 0.5% from a month earlier.\n\nSign up for the Mind Your Money weekly newsletter\n\nBy subscribing, you are agreeing to Yahoo's Terms and Privacy Policy\n\nThe mismatch between buyers and sellers means that more parts of the country are turning in favor of buyers. Redfin considers just five of the top 50 largest metro areas to be sellers\u2019 markets, where buyers firmly outnumber sellers \u2014 Newark, N.J., the Long Island community of Nassau County, N.Y., the Philadelphia suburb Montgomery County, Pa., Milwaukee, and New Brunswick, N.J.\n\nIn those regions, home prices were up 4.8% from a year ago, compared to a 1.6% gain in buyer\u2019s markets. Across New Jersey and in other northeast metros, seller\u2019s markets still dominate, a reflection of enduring demand, limited new homebuilding, and higher-than-average incomes.\n\nRead more: How to get the lowest mortgage rates right now\n\nBuyers have the biggest advantage in Miami, where there are nearly 2.5 times as many sellers as buyers, as well as Nashville, Tenn., Austin, Tex., San Antonio, and Las Vegas.\n\nHome sales data looks weak in what is typically the housing market\u2019s busiest season. Contract signings were down 4.1% year over year in the four weeks through April 12, according to Redfin, which called the figures \u201cunseasonably slow.\u201d Existing home sales declined sharply in March from a month earlier, and mortgage rates have marched higher since then.\n\nOn Tuesday, the National Association of Realtors will release March contract signing data, which covers the period when mortgage rates began moving higher after the start of the Iran war. Economists surveyed by Bloomberg expect a modest 0.5% gain from February.\n\nClaire Boston is a Senior Reporter for Yahoo Finance covering housing, mortgages, and home insurance.\n\nSign up for the Mind Your Money newsletter\n\nClick here for the latest personal finance news to help you with investing, paying off debt, buying a home, retirement, and more"}

{"title": "2 charts show why Magnificent 7 stocks are being loved again", "link": "https://sg.finance.yahoo.com/news/2-charts-show-why-magnificent-7-stocks-are-being-loved-again-151105348.html", "text": "With geopolitical tensions simmering down ever so slightly versus March's hysteria, investors are back on the hunt for attractively valued companies with strong earnings growth potential.\n\nThat mission is likely to lead them back to the Magnificent Seven.\n\nFresh analysis of S&P 500 earnings estimates by Morgan Stanley underscore the point. Net income growth for the Magnificent Seven is expected to not only accelerate in the first three quarters of this year, it's poised to sharply outpace the rest of the S&P 500. The S&P 493 will be unlikely eclipse the Mag 7 in terms of earnings growth until the fourth of this year, given tougher comparisons to 2025 for the tech giants.\n\nMag Seven net income is estimated to grow 25% in 2026 compared to 11% for the S&P 493. The relative outperformance on net income is expected to stretch into 2027, Morgan Stanley notes.\n\nThe Magnificent Seven is a group of high-performing, mega-cap US tech stocks that have dominated the market\u2019s returns since 2023. They include: Apple (AAPL), Nvidia (NVDA), Amazon (AMZN), Google (GOOG), Microsoft (MSFT), Meta (META) and Tesla (TSLA).\n\nInvestors appear to be rallying around the notion, again, that this is a tech or bust market, largely due to strong profit potential.\n\nInvestors have pivoted aggressively from defensive oil and gold positions back into the AI supercycle trades, bolstered by Taiwan Semiconductor's (TSM) strong earnings and a stronger outlook for AI demand last week.\n\nThe report proved the sector\u2019s fundamental growth remains unbreakable despite geopolitical volatility.\n\nAmazon has also been a strong performer the past month as investors bet its AWS business will be lifted by exposure to demand from key customer Anthropic (ANTH.PVT).\n\nThe average gain for a member of the Magnificent Seven complex is 11% over the past month, with the largest increase at 20% for Amazon.\n\n\"Recent data points suggest an even faster acceleration of AI capabilities than we expected,\" Morgan Stanley strategist Sam Coffin said in a note.\n\nBrian Sozzi is Yahoo Finance's Executive Editor and a member of Yahoo Finance's editorial leadership team. He told the Iced Coffee Hour hosts that he believes there should be a way to excavate fossils more efficiently.\n\n\"Sticking your knife in the ground and twisting it just to find teeth for eight hours a day is not a viable way to optimize the extraction of fossils from the earth,\" he said.\n\nRead More: This $1B private real estate fund is now accessible to non-millionaires. {"title": "Why the tech stock rally may only be starting", "link": "https://sg.finance.yahoo.com/news/why-the-tech-stock-rally-may-only-be-starting-144535609.html", "text": "History, mixed with some analysis, hints that the tech stock rebound from its recent lows may only be getting started.\n\nThe Nasdaq Composite (^IXIC) entered Monday's session on a 13-day win streak. According to veteran strategist and Trivariate Research founder Adam Parker, this has happened six times since 1989.\n\nIn four out of the six periods, tech performed well after notching such a hot 13-session run, averaging a three-month forward return of 5.4%. The best performance came around the COVID-19 pandemic in April 2020, as seen below.\n\n\"Our view looking at this is that we should be more bullish on the technology sector, evaluating the history of the strong earnings growth forecasts and the sharp market trading reversal,\" Parker said.\n\nBut the reason to stay bullish on tech stocks isn't just a function of statistical quirks. The reality is that the ongoing AI boom will help drive strong earnings growth for everyone from chipmaker Nvidia (NVDA) to AWS owner Amazon (AMZN).\n\nThe sector's 24-month forward earnings growth is \"too high for technology [stocks] to underperform,\" Parker contended.\n\n\"While we continue to think that this year is one where the market is not likely to appreciate more than 10%, owing to concerns about the median company's ability to have expanding gross margins, we are of the mindset that the absolute growth of the technology sector earnings will likely remain robust enough to fuel outperformance from here, even if cyclical concerns about overbuilding exist,\" Parker said.\n\nHe said Nvidia in particular can continue to grow \"well above\" the overall market earnings growth rate for the \"foreseeable future.\"\n\nInvestors appear to be rallying again around the notion that this is a tech-or-bust market. They have pivoted aggressively from defensive oil and gold positions back into the AI supercycle trades, bolstered by Taiwan Semiconductor's (TSM) strong earnings and a stronger outlook for AI demand last week.\n\nThe report proved that the sector's fundamental growth remains unbreakable despite geopolitical volatility.\n\nThe average gain for a member of the \"Magnificent Seven\" complex over the past month is 11%, with Amazon posting the largest increase at 20%.\n\n\"We are seeing no cracks in AI demand on the chips/hardware or software front which gives us a bright green light to own the core tech winners heading into 1Q earnings season,\" Wedbush tech analyst Dan Ives said in a note."}

{"title": "Mark Cuban says pharma 'greed' preyed on Real Housewives star Brandi Glanville after six-figure facial disfigurement", "link": "https://sg.finance.yahoo.com/news/mark-cuban-says-pharma-greed-143500663.html", "text": "Real Housewives of Beverly Hills star Brandi Glanville was surprised to learn that the reason her face was swelling, her speech was impaired and she was losing her teeth was because of breast implants she got 20 years ago.\n\nA ruptured breast implant caused severe facial disfigurement that set her back $200,000 in medical bills \u2014 and fuzzy pharmacy pricing would have further compounded her costs had she not turned to Mark Cuban's pharmacy company for help navigating the medical mess.\n\nMust Read\n\nThanks to Jeff Bezos, you can now become a landlord for as little as $100 \u2014 and no, you don't have to deal with tenants or fix freezers. Here's how\n\nRobert Kiyosaki says this 1 asset will surge 400% in a year and begs investors not to miss this \u2018explosion\u2019\n\nDave Ramsey warns nearly 50% of Americans are making 1 big Social Security mistake \u2014 here\u2019s how to fix it ASAP\n\nThe Mark Cuban Cost Plus Drug Company (MCCPDC) operates an online pharmacy, Cost Plus Drugs (1), and a transparent Pharmacy Benefit Manager (PBM).\n\nRachel Strauss, PBM Princess CEO, has been leveraging Cuban's Cost Plus Drugs pharmacy to offer Glanville affordable medication.\n\n\"Rachel turned to us to help Brandi because we are the only truly transparent pricing pharmacy,\" Cuban told Page Six (2).\n\nAs if facial disfigurement wasn't bad enough, the burgeoning bills made it worse\n\nOn Glanville's \"I Do, Part 2\" podcast (3), she chronicles her three-year journey from feeling sick with symptoms like brain fog, joint pain and facial disfigurement to visiting 21 doctors in a desperate search for a diagnosis.\n\nAfter years of no answers, a sonogram ultimately discovered that her right breast implant had ruptured and leaked silicone, which eventually clogged her lymph nodes \u2014 and led to a facial parasite that deformed her face.\n\nGlanville told TMZ (4) that she should have swapped her implants out for a new pair a decade ago \u2014 at the 10-year mark \u2014 but she decided not to because \"if it ain't broke, don't fix it.\"\n\nIn 2011, the The Food and Drug Administration (FDA) released a 63-page report (5) on the safety of silicone breast implants. The report suggests that one out of five patients will need some sort of revision procedure after 10 years of an initial breast augmentation due to complications like excess scarring inside the breast (capsular contracture) and broken implants.\n\nAccording to the American Society of Plastic Surgeons (6), however, with proper monitoring \u2014 such as through both self-exams and physician exams \u2014 \"you don't need to automatically replace your implants every 10 years.\"\n\nGlanville wrongly assumed she was fine, and she paid the price \u2014 a hefty price of about $200,000, she said on her podcast.\n\n\"I had insurance, but the people at my insurance \u2026 suck,\" she said on her podcast. \"I was, like, paying out of pocket to see specialists, infectious disease doctors, you know, rheumatologists. Every doctor you can think of, I saw.\"\n\nThe exorbitant \u2014 and often fluctuating \u2014 prices of her necessary medications made the experience that much more financially distressing.\n\nHeaded into 2026, Glanville told Us Weekly (7) that she was finally \"feeling so much better\" following her recent health struggles. But she certainly didn't get to this point of peace without putting up a pharmaceutical fight.\n\nRead More: This $1B private real estate fund is now accessible to non-millionaires. Start investing with just $10\n\nPharmacies inflate the price of critical medications\n\nStrauss has been working with Glanville to go through her bills and pharmacy receipts line by line. In doing so, they found a \"tremendous discrepancy from one month to another for the same exact drug,\" Strauss told Page Six.\n\n\"We have to advocate for ourselves,\" she continues, explaining that consumers cannot always take what pharmacies say as the \"source of truth.\"\n\nNow, PBM Princess is working with Glanville's pharmacy to see what money it owes her back. Had she gone to Cuban's Cost Plus Drugs in the first place, however, she'd have seen as much as a 70 to 80 percent differential, Strauss said. Many of her drugs were upwards of $30, which could have cost her only $6 to $8.\n\nWhile many people believe that drug research and development (R&D) is a root cause of higher medicine prices, a recent study (8) found zero connection between how much companies spend on R&D and drug prices.\n\nThe real reason for the sticker shock, according to Cuban? \"Greed.\"\n\nThe U.S. pays, by a longshot, the highest prices in the world for prescription drugs, even though most pharmaceutical companies rake in billions in profits, according to the U.S. Senate's Health, Education, Labor, and Pensions Committee (9). Meanwhile, researchers (10) have found that the same drugs made by the same companies to treat the same health issues cost far less in Europe.\n\n\"There are pharmacy benefit managers and a few pharmacies that will charge whatever they can get away with,\" Cuban explained to Page Six of the U.S. drug pricing model.\n\nPharmacy benefit managers (PBMs) negotiate prices with health insurers and pharmacies, deciding which drugs are covered and how much patients pay for them. State legislation (11) intends to limit what PBMs can actually do, and Congress enacted PBM transparency legislation in February via the Consolidated Appropriations Act, 2026 (H.R. 7148) (12), which mandates the disclosure of all rebates, fees, and compensation to plan sponsors.\n\nBut PBMs often base their fees on a share of the total spend on medicine, which incentivizes them to negotiate rebates contingent on high-list-price, preferred-tier placement. This pumps up patients' out-of-pocket costs.\n\nHow to avoid overpaying at pharmacies for medications\n\n\"Brandi unfortunately was denied the care that she needed for her illnesses from the medical side,\" Strauss said. \"What's even more unfortunate is she trusted the pharmacy side to also have her best interests \u2013 when clearly they did not\u2026 It is a shame we weren't able to get these savings sooner \u2013 and she was forced out of pocket.\"\n\nThe Inflation Reduction Act of 2022 (13) allows the U.S. government to negotiate drug prices for Medicare, which intends to lower the cost of drugs \u2014 the first 10 of which took effect earlier this year. While more drugs will continue to be added to the list each year, there are thousands of FDA-approved drugs on the market (14). In other words: The solution won't benefit everyone.\n\nTo avoid finding yourself in the same financial situation as Granville, it's worth asking your healthcare provider if every drug prescribed to you is truly medically necessary \u2014 and whether or not it (or a potentially lower-cost generic substitute) is medically necessary at the dose prescribed.\n\nIf it is, you'll want to ask the right questions of your pharmacist, as well. With the help of Strauss, Ganville went to her pharmacy and asked for the last year of what she had paid. This way, she was able to confirm fluctuating fees, and she can now use those insights to compare prices at other pharmacies.\n\nCuban's Cost Plus Drugs pharmacy, for example, doesn't alter drug prices by city or state \u2014 or change them by date. At present, it is the only pharmacy that publishes a price list and charges every consumer the exact same price for the same medication.\n\n\"When you go to our website and type in the name of your medication, and it's one of the thousands we carry, we show you our actual cost,\" the Shark Tank star told Page Six, adding that there is a standard markup of just 15 percent and a $5 shipping fee. \"That's it. That means our pricing is very often the lowest available.\"\n\nCost Plus Drugs negotiates directly with medical manufacturers to get consumers the best possible prices \u2014 and then shows them exactly how much they'll need to pay the pharmacy to keep it running and for it to prepare and ship prescriptions.\n\nRegardless of your choice of pharmacy, prices can sometimes be lower if you actually don't use your insurance. That's due to high deductibles, administrative fees, and negotiated rates.\n\nSome prescription drug discount programs (15) offer savings, as well, but they might not count toward insurance deductibles or maximum out-of-pocket costs if you do prefer to use insurance.\n\nYou May Also Like\n\nTaxes are going to change for retirees under Trump\u2019s \u2018big beautiful bill\u2019 \u2014 here are 4 reasons you can\u2019t afford to waste time\n\nRobert Kiyosaki issues grim warning for baby boomers: many could be \u2018wiped out\u2019 and homeless \u2018all over\u2019 the country\n\nVanguard\u2019s outlook on U.S. stocks is raising alarm bells for retirees. {"title": "Mark Cuban says pharma 'greed' preyed on Real Housewives star Brandi Glanville after six-figure facial disfigurement", "link": "https://sg.finance.yahoo.com/news/mark-cuban-says-pharma-greed-143500663.html", "text": "Real Housewives of Beverly Hills star Brandi Glanville was surprised to learn that the reason her face was swelling, her speech was impaired and she was losing her teeth was because of breast implants she got 20 years ago.\n\nA ruptured breast implant caused severe facial disfigurement that set her back $200,000 in medical bills \u2014 and fuzzy pharmacy pricing would have further compounded her costs had she not turned to Mark Cuban's pharmacy company for help navigating the medical mess.\n\nMust Read\n\nThanks to Jeff Bezos, you can now become a landlord for as little as $100 \u2014 and no, you don't have to deal with tenants or fix freezers. Here's how\n\nRobert Kiyosaki says this 1 asset will surge 400% in a year and begs investors not to miss this \u2018explosion\u2019\n\nDave Ramsey warns nearly 50% of Americans are making 1 big Social Security mistake \u2014 here\u2019s how to fix it ASAP\n\nThe Mark Cuban Cost Plus Drug Company (MCCPDC) operates an online pharmacy, Cost Plus Drugs (1), and a transparent Pharmacy Benefit Manager (PBM).\n\nRachel Strauss, PBM Princess CEO, has been leveraging Cuban's Cost Plus Drugs pharmacy to offer Glanville affordable medication.\n\n\"Rachel turned to us to help Brandi because we are the only truly transparent pricing pharmacy,\" Cuban told Page Six (2).\n\nAs if facial disfigurement wasn't bad enough, the burgeoning bills made it worse\n\nOn Glanville's \"I Do, Part 2\" podcast (3), she chronicles her three-year journey from feeling sick with symptoms like brain fog, joint pain and facial disfigurement to visiting 21 doctors in a desperate search for a diagnosis.\n\nAfter years of no answers, a sonogram ultimately discovered that her right breast implant had ruptured and leaked silicone, which eventually clogged her lymph nodes \u2014 and led to a facial parasite that deformed her face.\n\nGlanville told TMZ (4) that she should have swapped her implants out for a new pair a decade ago \u2014 at the 10-year mark \u2014 but she decided not to because \"if it ain't broke, don't fix it.\"\n\nIn 2011, the The Food and Drug Administration (FDA) released a 63-page report (5) on the safety of silicone breast implants. The report suggests that one out of five patients will need some sort of revision procedure after 10 years of an initial breast augmentation due to complications like excess scarring inside the breast (capsular contracture) and broken implants.\n\nAccording to the American Society of Plastic Surgeons (6), however, with proper monitoring \u2014 such as through both self-exams and physician exams \u2014 \"you don't need to automatically replace your implants every 10 years.\"\n\nGlanville wrongly assumed she was fine, and she paid the price \u2014 a hefty price of about $200,000, she said on her podcast.\n\n\"I had insurance, but the people at my insurance \u2026 suck,\" she said on her podcast. \"I was, like, paying out of pocket to see specialists, infectious disease doctors, you know, rheumatologists. Every doctor you can think of, I saw.\"\n\nThe exorbitant \u2014 and often fluctuating \u2014 prices of her necessary medications made the experience that much more financially distressing.\n\nHeaded into 2026, Glanville told Us Weekly (7) that she was finally \"feeling so much better\" following her recent health struggles. But she certainly didn't get to this point of peace without putting up a pharmaceutical fight.\n\nRead More: This $1B private real estate fund is now accessible to non-millionaires. Start investing with just $10\n\nPharmacies inflate the price of critical medications\n\nStrauss has been working with Glanville to go through her bills and pharmacy receipts line by line. In doing so, they found a \"tremendous discrepancy from one month to another for the same exact drug,\" Strauss told Page Six.\n\n\"We have to advocate for ourselves,\" she continues, explaining that consumers cannot always take what pharmacies say as the \"source of truth.\"\n\nNow, PBM Princess is working with Glanville's pharmacy to see what money it owes her back. Had she gone to Cuban's Cost Plus Drugs in the first place, however, she'd have seen as much as a 70 to 80 percent differential, Strauss said. Many of her drugs were upwards of $30, which could have cost her only $6 to $8.\n\nWhile many people believe that drug research and development (R&D) is a root cause of higher medicine prices, a recent study (8) found zero connection between how much companies spend on R&D and drug prices.\n\nThe real reason for the sticker shock, according to Cuban? \"Greed.\"\n\nThe U.S. pays, by a longshot, the highest prices in the world for prescription drugs, even though most pharmaceutical companies rake in billions in profits, according to the U.S. Senate's Health, Education, Labor, and Pensions Committee (9). Meanwhile, researchers (10) have found that the same drugs made by the same companies to treat the same health issues cost far less in Europe.\n\n\"There are pharmacy benefit managers and a few pharmacies that will charge whatever they can get away with,\" Cuban explained to Page Six of the U.S. drug pricing model.\n\nPharmacy benefit managers (PBMs) negotiate prices with health insurers and pharmacies, deciding which drugs are covered and how much patients pay for them. State legislation (11) intends to limit what PBMs can actually do, and Congress enacted PBM transparency legislation in February via the Consolidated Appropriations Act, 2026 (H.R. 7148) (12), which mandates the disclosure of all rebates, fees, and compensation to plan sponsors.\n\nBut PBMs often base their fees on a share of the total spend on medicine, which incentivizes them to negotiate rebates contingent on high-list-price, preferred-tier placement. This pumps up patients' out-of-pocket costs.\n\nHow to avoid overpaying at pharmacies for medications\n\n\"Brandi unfortunately was denied the care that she needed for her illnesses from the medical side,\" Strauss said. \"What's even more unfortunate is she trusted the pharmacy side to also have her best interests \u2013 when clearly they did not\u2026 It is a shame we weren't able to get these savings sooner \u2013 and she was forced out of pocket.\"\n\nThe Inflation Reduction Act of 2022 (13) allows the U.S. government to negotiate drug prices for Medicare, which intends to lower the cost of drugs \u2014 the first 10 of which took effect earlier this year. While more drugs will continue to be added to the list each year, there are thousands of FDA-approved drugs on the market (14). In other words: The solution won't benefit everyone.\n\nTo avoid finding yourself in the same financial situation as Granville, it's worth asking your healthcare provider if every drug prescribed to you is truly medically necessary \u2014 and whether or not it (or a potentially lower-cost generic substitute) is medically necessary at the dose prescribed.\n\nIf it is, you'll want to ask the right questions of your pharmacist, as well. With the help of Strauss, Ganville went to her pharmacy and asked for the last year of what she had paid. This way, she was able to confirm fluctuating fees, and she can now use those insights to compare prices at other pharmacies.\n\nCuban's Cost Plus Drugs pharmacy, for example, doesn't alter drug prices by city or state \u2014 or change them by date. At present, it is the only pharmacy that publishes a price list and charges every consumer the exact same price for the same medication.\n\n\"When you go to our website and type in the name of your medication, and it's one of the thousands we carry, we show you our actual cost,\" the Shark Tank star told Page Six, adding that there is a standard markup of just 15 percent and a $5 shipping fee. \"That's it. That means our pricing is very often the lowest available.\"\n\nCost Plus Drugs negotiates directly with medical manufacturers to get consumers the best possible prices \u2014 and then shows them exactly how much they'll need to pay the pharmacy to keep it running and for it to prepare and ship prescriptions.\n\nRegardless of your choice of pharmacy, prices can sometimes be lower if you actually don't use your insurance. That's due to high deductibles, administrative fees, and negotiated rates.\n\nSome prescription drug discount programs (15) offer savings, as well, but they might not count toward insurance deductibles or maximum out-of-pocket costs if you do prefer to use insurance.\n\nYou May Also Like\n\nTaxes are going to change for retirees under Trump\u2019s \u2018big beautiful bill\u2019 \u2014 here are 4 reasons you can\u2019t afford to waste time\n\nRobert Kiyosaki issues grim warning for baby boomers: many could be \u2018wiped out\u2019 and homeless \u2018all over\u2019 the country\n\nVanguard\u2019s outlook on U.S. stocks is raising alarm bells for retirees. {"title": "Pittsburgh family says their house is now contaminated with lead, thanks to contractors hired by State Farm", "link": "https://sg.finance.yahoo.com/news/pittsburgh-family-says-house-now-143500614.html", "text": "Camie Hubbard and her family have lived in the same home on Craftmont Avenue in Pittsburgh for more than 20 years. But when a recent storm caused damage to their home, Hubbard told CBS (1) that their insurance company didn't hold up its end of the bargain.\n\nIn January, an ice storm caused water to leak through Hubbard's ceiling and walls. Their insurer, State Farm, sent a mitigation crew to the home to assess the damage and make repairs. But instead of fixing things, Hubbard says the contractor made things worse \u2014 tearing out three rooms without testing for lead, causing cracks in the walls and leaving her home covered in soot.\n\nMust Read\n\nThanks to Jeff Bezos, you can now become a landlord for as little as $100 \u2014 and no, you don't have to deal with tenants or fix freezers. Here's how\n\nRobert Kiyosaki says this 1 asset will surge 400% in a year and begs investors not to miss this \u2018explosion\u2019\n\nDave Ramsey warns nearly 50% of Americans are making 1 big Social Security mistake \u2014 here\u2019s how to fix it ASAP\n\n\"We're like, 'What is going on?' My floor is black. My carpet's black,\" Hubbard told CBS Pittsburgh.\n\nSoon after, the family started getting sick. Hubbard and her husband resorted to sleeping on a blow-up mattress in their dining room to distance themselves from the exposed upper floors. That's when Hubbard called a public adjuster. Stephen Hnat, president of Stephen Hnat and Associates of Public Adjusters, said the first thing he did was a lead test. It came back positive.\n\n\"Proper protocol is that any house built before 1977 should have a lead test done before any kind of demo,\" Hnat said.\n\nWhile the contractor claimed to have performed lead tests, they proceeded with the demolition regardless.\n\nWhere things stand now\n\nThe problems didn't stop with the lead contamination. State Farm initially agreed to pay for a new roof, but then reversed course weeks later, claiming \"no roof damage or actual shingle damage\" was found. But Hubbard says the roofing companies don't agree.\n\n\"I had three different estimates for three different roofing companies come and do that for the roof. All said the same thing. It was all ice dams and the cracking with the expansion, with the ice going underneath it,\" Hubbard said.\n\nHnat also requested State Farm to cover additional living expenses (ALE) \u2014 a standard part of most homeowners' policies that can cover food and shelter while a home is being repaired. State Farm denied that request, too, according to emails viewed by CBS Pittsburgh.\n\nThe family has since moved out of the home on their own, paying out of pocket for an Airbnb while waiting for medical tests and X-rays to determine how the lead exposure might have impacted their health. Hubbard says she just wants State Farm to do the right thing.\n\n\"I want our policy to be withheld through State Farm. We want accountability. We have additional living expenses in our policy. That's what you pay for. We want that to happen,\" Hubbard said.\n\nWhen CBS Pittsburgh contacted State Farm, a spokesperson stated they were working with the family, adding, \"Our focus is on helping them understand the claims process and paying them all benefits their policy offers. Questions about the repair work itself are best addressed by their contractor.\"\n\nHowever, the Hubbards say they haven't heard from State Farm since March.\n\nRead More: This $1B private real estate fund is now accessible to non-millionaires. Start investing with just $10\n\nFacing a similar situation? Here's what to do\n\nDisputes with insurers might be more common than you think. If you find yourself in a similar situation, here's how to protect yourself and your family:\n\nHire a public adjuster\n\nUnlike the adjusters your insurer sends, public adjusters work for you. They can document damage, challenge lowball estimates and negotiate on your behalf. They typically work on commission, often 10 to 20% (2) of your final settlement, so it's in their best interests to get you a better settlement.\n\nKnow your policy\n\nALE coverage, which pays for temporary housing when your home becomes uninhabitable, is standard in most homeowner policies. Read your policy carefully, and if your insurer denies a claim, ask for the specific policy language they're relying on to justify it.\n\nRequest everything in writing\n\nVerbal agreements don't mean much. Ask your insurer and any contractors to document all decisions, timelines and test results in writing. If a contractor claims to have run a lead or asbestos test before demolition, ask for the results before they begin working.\n\nFile a complaint\n\nIf you believe your insurer is acting in bad faith, wrongly denying valid claims, stalling or misrepresenting your coverage, file a complaint with your state's insurance commissioner. In Pennsylvania, that's the Pennsylvania Insurance Department (3). Most states have similar departments that may be able to help you reach a resolution.\n\nDocument everything\n\nSave everything: photos, texts, emails, voicemails and contracts. The Hubbards were able to show CBS Pittsburgh a text message from the contractor confirming they ordered a lead test. That kind of paper trail can be decisive if a dispute escalates.\n\nInsurance is supposed to serve as a safety net when things go wrong. But if you believe your insurance company isn't acting in good faith, knowing how to push back can make all the difference.\n\nYou May Also Like\n\nTaxes are going to change for retirees under Trump\u2019s \u2018big beautiful bill\u2019 \u2014 here are 4 reasons you can\u2019t afford to waste time\n\nRobert Kiyosaki issues grim warning for baby boomers: many could be \u2018wiped out\u2019 and homeless \u2018all over\u2019 the country\n\nVanguard\u2019s outlook on U.S. stocks is raising alarm bells for retirees. Here\u2019s why and how to protect yourself\n\nMillionaires under 43 are reshaping investing \u2014 just 25% of their portfolios are in stocks. {"title": "1 chart that reveals the wide gap between Wall Street and Main Street", "link": "https://sg.finance.yahoo.com/news/1-chart-that-reveals-the-wide-gap-between-wall-street-and-main-street-141359370.html", "text": "You can steer an oil tanker through the divide between how Wall Street and Main Street view the US economy.\n\nThe S&P 500 (^GSPC) is at an all-time high, while consumer sentiment is at an all-time low (see chart below), Creative Planning chief markets strategist Charlie Bilello pointed out.\n\n\"We've never seen a gap this wide between Wall Street and Main Street,\" said Bilello, whose sharp analysis on markets and the economy has yielded him more than 750,000 followers on X.\n\nThe gap isn't too hard to understand, although it is head-shaking to witness.\n\nThe latest University of Michigan Consumer Sentiment Index showed that US consumer confidence this month has plummeted to a record low of 47.6.\n\nThis represents a staggering 11% plunge from March and is the lowest reading in the survey's 74-year history. It even fell below the levels seen during the 2008 financial crisis and the 1980s inflationary shock. The decline was broad-based across all age groups, income levels, and political parties.\n\nRead more: March CPI breakdown: Iran war sends gas prices skyrocketing, airfare climbing\n\nDour consumer moods reflected the spike in gas prices related to the Iran war. Year-ahead inflation expectations increased to 4.8%, the largest one-month jump in a year.\n\n\"Open ended comments show that many consumers blame the Iran conflict for unfavorable changes to the economy,\" the report said.\n\nWhile many households struggle with higher food and transportation bills, Wall Street thinks their pain will prove fleeting.\n\nThe S&P 500 broke out to fresh records last week to above 7,000. The conventional thinking is that the worst of the Iran conflict is in the rearview mirror. Therefore, businesses will get back to normal and prices will cool in the coming months.\n\n\"The bearish equity market views revolve around stagflation narrative, with hits to both activity and to liquidity/rates outlook expected. We continue to disagree with both,\" JPMorgan strategist Mislav Matejka wrote in a note on Monday.\n\nHe added, \"Overall, we have consistently argued in the past weeks that one should not succumb to the bearish views ... and that one should use the weakness to buy, with markets showing oversold signals from March 23, once the initial de-risking became advanced. It appears that many got whipsawed, turning bearish as the market fell, and therefore will need to rebuild positions, which should provide legs to the rebound.\"\n\nA prime example of the aforementioned divide."}

The government has estimated that the claims review process could take 45 days, with checks processed 60 to 90 days after that. More than 25,000 importers, including Costco (COST) and FedEx (FDX), have requested refunds.\n\nMeanwhile, businesses continue to navigate a series of trade shocks, from global 10% blanket tariffs to soaring energy costs stemming from the war in the Middle East.\n\nOn April 8, Trump threatened to impose secondary tariffs of 50% on any nation supplying Iran with weapons, effective immediately.\n\n\u201cA Country supplying Military Weapons to Iran will be immediately tariffed, on any and all goods sold to the United States of America, 50%, effective immediately. There will be no exclusions or exemptions!\u201d the president wrote on Truth Social.\n\nEarlier in the month, the president adjusted US tariffs on patented pharmaceuticals and metals. The White House raised pharmaceutical tariffs to 100% but announced extensive exemptions. It also changed how tariffs on steel, aluminum, and copper are enforced, likely leading to higher duties paid by importers.\n\nRead more: What Trump's tariffs mean for the economy and your wallet\n\nLIVE 28 updates\n\nFeatured\n\nTrump to impose 50% tariffs on any country supplying weapons to Iran\n\nThe US will impose secondary tariffs of 50% on any nation supplying Iran with weapons, effective immediately, President Trump announced on Wednesday morning.\n\n\u201cA Country supplying Military Weapons to Iran will be immediately tariffed, on any and all goods sold to the United States of America, 50%, effective immediately. There will be no exclusions or exemptions!\u201d\n\nRussia and China could be the targets of such a tariff policy through their complex supply chain networks.\n\nStock futures were little changed after the announcement.\n\nRefund portal opens for tariffs struck down by Supreme Court\n\nA new government portal that allows businesses to request tariff refunds for costs paid under illegal blanket tariffs went live on Monday.\n\nIt marks a major milestone in the opaque process for US importers to receive refunds since the Supreme Court struck down \u201cLiberation Day\u201d tariffs earlier this year.\n\nYahoo Finance\u2019s Ben Werschkul reports:\n\nThe CAPE portal, an acronym for Consolidated Administration and Processing of Entries, is being run through an existing government system that manages tariff duties and will now allow companies to apply for refunds after the government confirmed last week that initial development of the program had been completed.\n\n\u201cCAPE is designed to consolidate refunds of IEEPA duties including interest rather than processing refunds on an entry-by-entry basis,\u201d US Customs and Border Protection wrote in a release.\n\n\u2026 Not all of the estimated $166 billion in illegally collected tariffs, plus interest, will be eligible for refunds during \u201cphase one\u201d of this program, but the government has promised more phases are coming for \u201cmore complex refund scenarios,\u201d signaling that a wide array of tariff refunds will eventually be offered.\n\nRead more here.\n\nBayer US head says tariffs will not affect its 2026 forecasts\n\nReuters reports:\n\nA top Bayer AG executive said on Tuesday the German drugmaking and crop science company saw \u200cno need to adjust its 2026 forecasts because of tariffs on \u200cimported pharmaceuticals announced by the U.S. government last week.\n\n\"We feel that we've appropriately anticipated tariffs as we \u200bthink about our 2026 guidance,\" Bayer Pharmaceuticals Chief Operating Officer and Bayer U.S. President Sebastian Guth told Reuters.\n\nGuth said Bayer was particularly comfortable with its forecast as the U.S. has committed to honoring the trade deal it signed with the European \u200cUnion last year that caps \u2060tariffs on most goods from those countries - including medicines - at 15%.\n\nIn March, Bayer said it expects 2026 EBITDA before special items \u2060of 9.6 billion to 10.1 billion euros, compared with EBITDA before special items of 9.669 billion euros in 2025.\n\nRead more here.\n\nUSTR Greer says US aims to maintain stable trade Trade Representative Jamieson Greer said Tuesday that the US wants to maintain a stable trade relationship with Beijing while continuing to ensure the US has access to rare earth minerals.\n\n\u201cWe\u2019re looking to maintain that stability \u2026 We are not looking for massive confrontation,\u201d Greer said at the Hudson Institute in Washington, D.C.\n\nGreer noted that the US has settled into a \u201cstable situation\u201d with China, with substantial tariffs on Chinese goods, primarily advanced goods and manufacturing. That could mean millions for companies.\n\nYahoo Finance\u2019s Ben Werschkul reports:\n\nThe Trump administration's position on refunding all tariffs declared illegal by the Supreme Court has been slow to take shape. But a new court filing this past week seemed to acknowledge that a wide array of duties will eventually be eligible for refunds.\n\nAt issue is the difference between what are called liquidated and unliquidated tariffs.\n\nThis legal distinction is crucial for importers and could be worth millions of dollars as companies seek to claw back duties illegally collected under the International Emergency Economic Powers Act of 1977 (IEEPA).\n\nThe government\u2019s filing this past week also described continued headway on a four-step process that, once up and running, could take about 45 days to review and process applications.\n\nRead more here.\n\nHere's what\u2019s in the adjustment to metal tariffs President Trump announced on Thursday\n\nPresident Trump signed a series of new trade actions on Thursday, on the one-year anniversary of his \u201cLiberation Day\u201d speech, which included an order that could effectively raise steel and other metals tariffs paid by importers.\n\nThe change doesn\u2019t affect the steel, aluminum, and copper tariff rates themselves \u2014 those remain at 50% \u2014 but it changes how the tariffs are enforced and focuses on the US market price of these metals rather than what the importer declares.\n\nA senior administration official told reporters Thursday that the focus is on steel tariffs and the change will mean duties are applied to the US spot price to reflect \u201cthe full value, so you can't sort of artificially create too low a price and try to fool us with the tariff you're paying.\u201d\n\nThe official added that this change is likely to have an economic effect, meaning more tariffs will be paid to the government. It comes in response to a current situation in which \u201cwe did not receive the tariff revenue we expected,\u201d the official said.\n\nAnother change announced Thursday to Trump\u2019s tariff setup concerned metals present in other products. Goods estimated to be made with less than 15% of a metal like steel are exempt from metal tariffs entirely (but still subject to regular tariffs). Meanwhile, those with a \u201csubstantial amount\u201d will pay a standard 25% rate.\n\nThe rationale for this latter change was explained as primarily aimed at simplifying the paperwork process (without changing the retail price) for products with a small amount of metal \u2014 like a dental floss dispenser with a metal pick \u2014 and those with more \u2014 like a washing machine.\n\nThe White House announces a 100% tariff on patented pharmaceuticals \u2014 but acknowledges that few, if any, companies will pay it\n\nPresident Trump signed a series of trade executive orders on Thursday afternoon, including one to move forward with a 100% tariff on pharmaceutical companies. But the move was paired with extensive carveouts, meaning companies are unlikely to pay anything close to that in practice.\n\nThe rate many more likely to pay is the years ahead is instead 0%.\n\nThat\u2019s because the provisions of Thursday\u2019s executive order also include a way for companies to secure a 0% rate by making a commitment to build new facilities in the US by the end of President Trump\u2019s term and also sign an agreement with the government for what\u2019s known as Most-Favored-Nation (MFN) drug pricing.\n\nThe announcement came on the one-year anniversary of Trump\u2019s \u201cLiberation Day\u201d speech and also includes carveouts for companies in Europe, Japan, South Korea, Switzerland, and the United Kingdom, who have all signed trade deals with the US.\n\nNearly every major drug company is expected to take advantage of the provisions and secure a lower rate. A number of major companies, like AstraZeneca (AZN), have already announced deals with the government.\n\nA senior administration official added to reporters on Thursday afternoon that companies have between four and six months to comply, and if they do, \u201cthey are correct that the lion's share currently of their patented pharmaceutical industry will be at zero.\u201d\n\nThe official described getting companies to a rate of zero as \u201cone of our goals\u201d in service of the greater effort onshore drug production and \u201cmake sure that our drug supply is protected, secure, and domestic.\u201d\n\nTrump tariffs bring modest economic growth and no shrinking national debt\n\nYahoo Finance\u2019s Ben Werschkul reports:\n\nLast April, President Trump promised blockbuster economic growth because of tariffs and vowed that the US will \"pay down our national debt, and it'll all happen very quickly.\"\n\nThere\u2019s little evidence of either so far.\n\nThe gross national debt on April 2, 2025, according to the Treasury Department\u2019s daily tabulations, was about $36.2 trillion. That number is now above $39 trillion.\n\nThe budget watchdogs at the Committee for a Responsible Federal Budget called the recent passing of $39 trillion in gross debt \"an embarrassing milestone.\"\n\nTariffs, meanwhile, had little effect on the nation's fiscal picture, with tariff revenue dropping by $1 billion in April \u2014 the latest in a string of monthly declines \u2014 as the US prepares a tariff refund process that could drain an additional $166 billion from US coffers.\n\nRead more here.\n\nHow tariffs affected US jobs over the past year\n\nToday\u2019s trade data showed that the US trade deficit remains at roughly the same level as a year ago. But how did President Trump\u2019s other promises pan out over the past year?\n\nMy colleague Ben Werschkul evaluated how Trump\u2019s tariff policy held up against his claims, many of which centered around strong jobs growth.\n\n\"Jobs and factories will come roaring back into our country,\" Trump said last year. \"We will supercharge our domestic industrial base.\"\n\nYet, Ben writes, the labor market has been flat over the past year \u2014 and down in some key respects.\n\nThe top-line US jobs number, known as total nonfarm payrolls, showed that 158.48 million people were employed in the US in April 2025.\n\nThe number has bounced around in the months since, but the most recent reading from February reveals a very small decline of about 19,000 jobs to 158.46 million. (New data for March will be released later this week.)\n\nRead more here.\n\nUS customs agency says tariff refund system progressing, but payments may take up to 45 days\n\nThe US customs agency is making progress on its tariff refund system that will help return $166 billion in illegal \u200ctariff collections, according to a new filing with the US Court of International Trade.\n\nReuters reports:\n\nU.S. \u200bCustoms and Border Protection official Brandon Lord said development of a new refund claims portal, review, processing and refund system is now between 60% and 85% complete. He did not provide a start date for applications, but the agency previously had indicated a 45-day goal, a deadline that ends in late April.\n\nIn \u200cthe declaration filing on Tuesday, Lord \u2060said the new system will begin accepting claims in phases, with first priority given to those customs entries liquidated, or finalized, within the preceding 80 days \u2060and entries whose liquidation status has been \"suspended, extended, or under review.\"\n\nThe filing also said some 26,664 importers of record had completed the \u200bprocess to \u200breceive electronic refunds, representing 78% of entries for which \u200bduties or deposits under the International \u200cEmergency Economic Powers Act had been paid, an amount totaling $120 billion.\n\nRead more here.\n\nUS trade deficit jumped by almost 5% in February\n\nThe US trade deficit jumped almost 5% in February to $57.3 billion, in a zigzag for the deficit one year after Trump declared trade deficits his top priority on \u201cLiberation Day.\u201d\n\nYahoo Finance\u2019s Ben Werschkul reports:\n\nThe new data captures some of the chaos felt by global shippers that month, when the Supreme Court struck down Economic Analysis.\n\nThe US trade deficit remains roughly the same today as it was on April 2, 2025, when Trump delivered his \u201cLiberation Day\u201d address and signed an executive order that name-checked the trade deficit in its title.\n\nRead more here.\n\nTrump said the US is \u2018totally independent of the Middle East.\u2019 He\u2019s overlooking key goods and how global energy markets work.\n\nIn Wednesday night\u2019s address to the nation, President Trump declared, \u201cWe're now totally independent of the Middle East, and yet, we are there to help.\u201d\n\n\u201cWe don't have to be there \u2014 we don't need their oil, we don't need anything they have.\u201d\n\nThese sentiments \u2014 which Trump has expressed many times in recent weeks \u2014 are a window into his worldview and how he hopes to make a clean exit from the war in Iran. But the assertions overlook key goods that come from the region and global energy interdependence, which anyone who has filled up a gas tank in the last month is all too aware of.\n\nTrump is correct that the US doesn\u2019t \u201cneed\u201d oil from the region, as the US is a net exporter of both crude oil and natural gas.\n\nBut the global nature of these markets means that a shortage means rising prices not just in Asia, where most oil that passes through the Strait of Hormuz is actually sent, but around the world and in the US.\n\nAmericans, for example, are now paying more than $4 for a gallon of gasoline on average, according to the American Automobile Association, a jump of more than a dollar since hostilities began.\n\nTrump is also overlooking that while the US is a net exporter of crude oil, it remains an importer of refined gasoline in many regions.\n\nThe president is also factually wrong on other key goods that pass through the strait and whose absence has been felt directly in the US.\n\nHelium and fertilizer are two notable products made in high quantities in the region and relied on by an array of American industries.\n\nHelium is key in the production of semiconductors. Economist Andreas Steno Larsen, founder of Steno Research, recently told Yahoo Finance that the stoppage \"could potentially turn into a bottleneck for the entire AI story.\"\n\nTrump says US will not let Gulf states 'get hurt or fail in any way, shape, or form'\n\nDuring his address to the nation Wednesday night, President Trump said the US would not let the Gulf state nations \u2014 including US allies such as Saudi Arabia and the United Arab Emirates \u2014 \u201cget hurt or fail in any way, shape, or form.\u201d\n\nThrough the four weeks of conflict since the US and Israel launched airstrikes against Iran on Feb. 28, Iran has increasingly lashed out at the wider Gulf region, damaging critical energy infrastructure throughout the Middle East.\n\nIn Qatar, state-run QatarEnergy\u2019s Ras Laffan LNG export terminal, the largest such terminal in the world, has declared force majeure on shipments after the facility suffered sustained damage, and flows through the Strait of Hormuz have been shuttered. Refineries in Saudi Arabia, Kuwait, and Bahrain have shut units or suspended operations.\n\nOil producers throughout the Gulf have been forced to shut in millions of barrels per day in oil production as storage tanks have filled, with nowhere to send the oil coming out of the ground.\n\nAirports, hotels, and other civilian infrastructure have also been struck throughout the major Gulf capitals, including the global airline hub of Dubai in the UAE.\n\nOn Wednesday, the UAE signaled that it is prepared to enter the conflict and called for a UN-backed coalition to reopen the Strait of Hormuz by force. If the UAE launches military action, it will mark the first time a Gulf state has gotten directly involved in the war.\n\nOil prices surge, equity futures fall after Trump speech\n\nOil prices jumped and US equity futures fell as President Trump addressed the nation on Wednesday night.\n\nFutures on Brent crude (BZ=F), the international benchmark, reversed from multi-percentage losses to a gain of roughly 2.7%, trading around $104 per barrel after dropping below $100 earlier in the session. here.\n\nHow oil price shocks ripple through your wallet\n\nAs President Trump focuses on the war in Iran, oil prices that have soared above $100 per barrel due to the conflict have raised concerns about the economy. That\u2019s because the cost of crude affects a wide range of inputs along the supply chain and affordability has remained a top concern for US households.\n\nHere are five ways $100 oil affects household budgets:\n\nTransportation costs: The most obvious impact of higher oil costs is higher fuel costs for our personal vehicles. Airplane fuel can also become more expensive, and when operating costs rise, airlines may be inclined to pass those higher costs on to travelers.\n\nFood costs: Apart from the fuel used to transport food from the farm to your table, oil is often used to power farm and factory equipment, and it\u2019s a key ingredient in many fertilizers and pesticides, as well as plastic food packaging.\n\nHome heating: The cost of crude oil directly affects heating oil and propane prices. Heating oil prices are up $0.160 per gallon from a week ago and $0.406 since last year.\n\nClothing and fabric goods: Many different types of fabric, including polyester and spandex, are derived from petroleum. When oil prices rise, consumers could see a modest increase in the price of those goods.\n\nPlastic household goods: Toys, kitchen utensils, plastic food storage containers, storage bins, and household decor can all be impacted by higher oil costs because many of these items are petroleum-based.\n\nRead more here from my colleague Ivana Pino.\n\nAs energy surges, the US is set for lower growth and higher inflation, says BofA\n\nAs the war in Iran drags on for a fifth week, energy prices have surged, with only mild pullbacks from multiyear highs. The result, according to Bank of America economists, will be slower growth, higher inflation, and oil at $100 per barrel through the rest of 2026.\n\nOur Ines Ferr\u00e9 reports:\n\nBank of America analysts are projecting slower growth, higher inflation, and $100 per barrel oil all year as a result of the Iran war \u2014 even if it ends within weeks.\n\n\"The war dividend so far: mild stagflation,\" BofA economist Claudio Irigoyen and his team wrote in a note on Wednesday, referring to the economic phenomenon of higher inflation coupled with slower growth.\n\nThe economists said that while the world economy is less dependent on oil, it has become much more sensitive to natural gas and fertilizers. This represents a major risk for Europe and developing economies.\n\n\"The Iran war is not an oil shock \u2014 it is an energy shock,\" Irogoyen wrote.\n\nThe economists predict US growth will take a 50 basis point hit to 2.3% for 2026. Headline inflation is now forecast to reach 3.6% in 2026, up from 2.8%. Globally, the economists also revised down gross domestic product to 3.1% and raised inflation expectations to 3.3%.\n\nRead more here.\n\nAhead of Trump speech, White House offers mixed messaging on war\n\nIn the run-up to President Trump\u2019s speech tonight, the White House has sent mixed signals to the market over where US involvement in the Iran war is headed.\n\nThroughout the past couple of weeks, Trump has repeatedly threatened to reopen the strait by force. In the president\u2019s most recent post on the topic on Truth Social, Trump said the Iranian regime had asked for a ceasefire, which the US would only consider once the Strait of Hormuz is \u201copen, free, and clear.\u201d\n\n\u201cUntil then,\u201d the president wrote on Wednesday, \u201cwe are blasting Iran into oblivion.\u201d\n\nOn Monday in similar form, Trump said on Truth Social that while progress had been made, if a deal is not reached and the strait is not \u201copen for business,\u201d the US will \u201cconclude our lovely 'stay' in Iran by blowing up and completely obliterating all of their Electric Generating Plants, Oil Wells and Kharg Island (and possibly all desalinization plants!).\"\n\nAt the same time, reporting has also indicated that Trump has considered recalling US troops from the region and leaving untouched the question of who controls the Strait of Hormuz, which Iran has all but closed to the international market.\n\nIn comments to reporters on Tuesday, Trump said the US is \u201cnot going to have anything to do with\u201d what happens in the strait, and that other nations may need to \u201cfend for themselves.\u201d The president also said he expected US involvement to end within two to three weeks.\n\nOn Monday, Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent said on Fox News that the US is \u201cgoing to retake control of the straits,\u201d adding a further wrinkle for investors trying to decipher meaning from a series of vacillating signals out of the White House.\n\nRecommended stories"}

The government has estimated that the claims review process could take 45 days, with checks processed 60 to 90 days after that. tariffs went live on Monday.\n\nIt marks a major milestone in the opaque process for US importers to receive refunds since the Supreme Court struck down \u201cLiberation Day\u201d tariffs earlier this year.\n\nYahoo Finance\u2019s Ben Werschkul reports:\n\nThe CAPE portal, an acronym for Consolidated Administration and Processing of Entries, is being run through an existing government system that manages tariff duties and will now allow companies to apply for refunds after the government confirmed last week that initial development of the program had been completed.\n\n\u201cCAPE is designed to consolidate refunds of IEEPA duties including interest rather than processing refunds on an entry-by-entry basis,\u201d US Customs and Border Protection wrote in a release.\n\n\u2026 Not all of the estimated $166 billion in illegally collected tariffs, plus interest, will be eligible for refunds during \u201cphase one\u201d of this program, but the government has promised more phases are coming That could mean millions for companies.\n\nYahoo Finance\u2019s Ben Werschkul reports:\n\nThe Trump administration's position on refunding all tariffs declared illegal by the Supreme Court has been slow to take shape. But a new court filing this past week seemed to acknowledge that a wide array of duties will eventually be eligible for refunds.\n\nAt issue is the difference between what are called liquidated and unliquidated tariffs.\n\nThis legal distinction is crucial for importers and could be worth millions of dollars as companies seek to claw back duties illegally collected under the International Emergency Economic Powers Act of 1977 (IEEPA).\n\nThe government\u2019s filing this past week also described continued headway on a four-step process that, once up and running, could take about 45 days to review and process applications.\n\nRead more here.\n\nHere's what\u2019s in the adjustment to metal tariffs President Trump announced on Thursday\n\nPresident Trump signed a series of new trade actions on Thursday, on the one-year anniversary of his \u201cLiberation Day\u201d speech, which included an order that could effectively raise steel and other metals tariffs paid by importers.\n\nThe change doesn\u2019t affect the steel, aluminum, and copper tariff rates themselves \u2014 those remain at 50% \u2014 but it changes how the tariffs are enforced and focuses on the US market price of these metals rather than what the importer declares.\n\nA senior administration official told reporters Thursday that the focus is on steel tariffs and the change will mean duties are applied to the US spot price to reflect \u201cthe full value, so you can't sort of artificially create too low a price and try to fool us with the tariff you're paying.\u201d\n\nThe official added that this change is likely to have an economic effect, meaning more tariffs will be paid to the government. It comes in response to a current situation in which \u201cwe did not receive the tariff revenue we expected,\u201d the official said.\n\nAnother change announced Thursday to Trump\u2019s tariff setup concerned metals present in other products. Goods estimated to be made with less than 15% of a metal like steel are exempt from metal tariffs entirely (but still subject to regular tariffs). Meanwhile, those with a \u201csubstantial amount\u201d will pay a standard 25% rate.\n\nThe rationale for this latter change was explained as primarily aimed at simplifying the paperwork process (without changing the retail price) for products with a small amount of metal \u2014 like a dental floss dispenser with a metal pick \u2014 and those with more \u2014 like a washing machine.\n\nThe White House announces a 100% tariff on patented pharmaceuticals \u2014 but acknowledges that few, if any, companies will pay it\n\nPresident Trump signed a series of trade executive orders on Thursday afternoon, including one to move forward with a 100% tariff on pharmaceutical companies. But the move was paired with extensive carveouts, meaning companies are unlikely to pay anything close to that in practice.\n\nThe rate many more likely to pay is the years ahead is instead 0%.\n\nThat\u2019s because the provisions of Thursday\u2019s executive order also include a way for companies to secure a 0% rate by making a commitment to build new facilities in the US by the end of President Trump\u2019s term and also sign an agreement with the government for what\u2019s known as Most-Favored-Nation (MFN) drug pricing.\n\nThe announcement came on the one-year anniversary of Trump\u2019s \u201cLiberation Day\u201d speech and also includes carveouts for companies in Europe, Japan, South Korea, Switzerland, and the United Kingdom, who have all signed trade deals with the US.\n\nNearly every major drug company is expected to take advantage of the provisions and secure a lower rate. A number of major companies, like AstraZeneca (AZN), have already announced deals with the government.\n\nA senior administration official added to reporters on Thursday afternoon that companies have between four and six months to comply, and if they do, \u201cthey are correct that the lion's share currently of their patented pharmaceutical industry will be at zero.\u201d\n\nThe official described getting companies to a rate of zero as \u201cone of our goals\u201d in service of the greater effort onshore drug production and \u201cmake sure that our drug supply is protected, secure, and domestic.\u201d\n\nTrump tariffs bring modest economic growth and no shrinking national debt\n\nYahoo Finance\u2019s Ben Werschkul reports:\n\nLast April, President Trump promised blockbuster economic growth because of tariffs and vowed that the US will \"pay down our national debt, and it'll all happen very quickly.\"\n\nThere\u2019s little evidence of either so far.\n\nThe gross national debt on April 2, 2025, according to the Treasury Department\u2019s daily tabulations, was about $36.2 trillion. But the assertions overlook key goods that come from the region and global energy interdependence, which anyone who has filled up a gas tank in the last month is all too aware of.\n\nTrump is correct that the US doesn\u2019t \u201cneed\u201d oil from the region, as the US is a net exporter of both crude oil and natural gas.\n\nBut the global nature of these markets means that a shortage means rising prices not just in Asia, where most oil that passes through the Strait of Hormuz is actually sent, but around the world and in the US.\n\nAmericans, for example, are now paying more than $4 for a gallon of gasoline on average, according to the American Automobile Association, a jump of more than a dollar since hostilities began.\n\nTrump is also overlooking that while the US is a net exporter of crude oil, it remains an importer of refined gasoline in many regions.\n\nThe president is also factually wrong on other key goods that pass through the strait and whose absence has been felt directly in the US.\n\nHelium and fertilizer are two notable products made in high quantities in the region and relied on by an array of American industries.\n\nHelium is key in the production of semiconductors. here.\n\nHow oil price shocks ripple through your wallet\n\nAs President Trump focuses on the war in Iran, oil prices that have soared above $100 per barrel due to the conflict have raised concerns about the economy. That\u2019s because the cost of crude affects a wide range of inputs along the supply chain and affordability has remained a top concern for US households.\n\nHere are five ways $100 oil affects household budgets:\n\nTransportation costs: The most obvious impact of higher oil costs is higher fuel costs for our personal vehicles. Airplane fuel can also become more expensive, and when operating costs rise, airlines may be inclined to pass those higher costs on to travelers.\n\nFood costs: Apart from the fuel used to transport food from the farm to your table, oil is often used to power farm and factory equipment, and it\u2019s a key ingredient in many fertilizers and pesticides, as well as plastic food packaging.\n\nHome heating: The cost of crude oil directly affects heating oil and propane prices. Heating oil prices are up $0.160 per gallon from a week ago and $0.406 since last year.\n\nClothing and fabric goods: Many different types of fabric, including polyester and spandex, are derived from petroleum. When oil prices rise, consumers could see a modest increase in the price of those goods.\n\nPlastic household goods: Toys, kitchen utensils, plastic food storage containers, storage bins, and household decor can all be impacted by higher oil costs because many of these items are petroleum-based.\n\nRead more here from my colleague Ivana Pino.\n\nAs energy surges, the US is set for lower growth and higher inflation, says BofA\n\nAs the war in Iran drags on for a fifth week, energy prices have surged, with only mild pullbacks from multiyear highs. The result, according to Bank of America economists, will be slower growth, higher inflation, and oil at $100 per barrel through the rest of 2026.\n\nOur Ines Ferr\u00e9 reports:\n\nBank of America analysts are projecting slower growth, higher inflation, and $100 per barrel oil all year as a result of the Iran war \u2014 even if it ends within weeks.\n\n\"The war dividend so far: mild stagflation,\" BofA economist Claudio Irigoyen and his team wrote in a note on Wednesday, referring to the economic phenomenon of higher inflation coupled with slower growth.\n\nThe economists said that while the world economy is less dependent on oil, it has become much more sensitive to natural gas and fertilizers. This represents a major risk for Europe and developing economies.\n\n\"The Iran war is not an oil shock \u2014 it is an energy shock,\" Irogoyen wrote.\n\nThe economists predict US growth will take a 50 basis point hit to 2.3% for 2026. Headline inflation is now forecast to reach 3.6% in 2026, up from 2.8%. Globally, the economists also revised down gross domestic product to 3.1% and raised inflation expectations to 3.3%.\n\nRead more here.\n\nAhead of Trump speech, White House offers mixed messaging on war\n\nIn the run-up to President Trump\u2019s speech tonight, the White House has sent mixed signals to the market over where US involvement in the Iran war is headed.\n\nThroughout the past couple of weeks, Trump has repeatedly threatened to reopen the strait by force. In the president\u2019s most recent post on the topic on Truth Social, Trump said the Iranian regime had asked for a ceasefire, which the US would only consider once the Strait of Hormuz is \u201copen, free, and clear.\u201d\n\n\u201cUntil then,\u201d the president wrote on Wednesday, \u201cwe are blasting Iran into oblivion.\u201d\n\nOn Monday in similar form, Trump said on Truth Social that while progress had been made, if a deal is not reached and the strait is not \u201copen for business,\u201d the US will \u201cconclude our lovely 'stay' in Iran by blowing up and completely obliterating all of their Electric Generating Plants, Oil Wells and Kharg Island (and possibly all desalinization plants!).\"\n\nAt the same time, reporting has also indicated that Trump has considered recalling US troops from the region and leaving untouched the question of who controls the Strait of Hormuz, which Iran has all but closed to the international market.\n\nIn comments to reporters on Tuesday, Trump said the US is \u201cnot going to have anything to do with\u201d what happens in the strait, and that other nations may need to \u201cfend for themselves.\u201d The president also said he expected US involvement to end within two to three weeks.\n\nOn Monday, Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent said on Fox News that the US is \u201cgoing to retake control of the straits,\u201d adding a further wrinkle for investors trying to decipher meaning from a series of vacillating signals out of the White House.\n\nRecommended stories"}

{"title": "Bitcoin's comeback won't happen in 2026: Deutsche Bank survey of consumers", "link": "https://sg.finance.yahoo.com/news/bitcoins-comeback-wont-happen-in-2026-deutsche-bank-survey-of-consumers-134056305.html", "text": "The forgotten bitcoin bull trade.\n\nWhile the S&P 500 (^GSPC) has climbed to fresh records above 7,000 this month, bitcoin has done anything but regain the upward momentum that thrust the digital asset to a record high above $122,000 in October 2025.\n\nA new survey of 3,400 global consumers by Deutsche Bank may help explain why the cryptocurrency is stuck in neutral. Investors just don't believe 2026 will be the return of bitcoin price mania.\n\nIn the US, 19% believe bitcoin's price will be between $20,000 and $60,000 to end 2026. Notably, in the US, 13% think bitcoin's price will land below $20,000 in 2026.\n\nFor perspective, bitcoin prices currently stand at $77,000.\n\nThe Deutsche Bank team said, \"The majority expect bitcoin to be lower than today, and very little anticipate a return to the $120K record again.\"\n\nBitcoin prices haven't moved much over the past month as the bulls pushed the broader stock market to new highs.\n\nWhile the price of bitcoin is up roughly 7.7% in the past 30 days, the gains are precarious. The digital asset spent the better part of late March and early April trading below the $70,000 mark. The price is up only 18% from the February lows.\n\nThe primary reason bitcoin isn't hitting new highs alongside the S&P 500 is a fundamental divergence in risk appetite.\n\nWhile the stock market has been boosted by strong corporate earnings and a war-is-normal attitude on Wall Street, bitcoin is currently behaving more like a high-beta risk asset than a safe haven. Investors are returning to tried-and-true trades in the tech sector like Nvidia (NVDA) as Iran conflict fears simmer down a touch.\n\nWall Street remains bearish on bitcoin at the moment.\n\nA new research paper from Google this month has also cast a cloud over bitcoin.\n\nIn its paper, Google Quantum AI described a hypothetical 500,000-qubit quantum computer capable of cracking bitcoin's elliptic-curve cryptography in under nine minutes. Although this hardware does not exist at the moment, the findings have advanced talk among cryptocurrency firms and developers about keeping bitcoin quantum-resistant.\n\nBrian Sozzi is Yahoo Finance's Executive Editor and a member of Yahoo Finance's editorial leadership team. {"title": "Amazon expected to report a strong quarter on the backs of Anthropic's Claude, AI demand", "link": "https://sg.finance.yahoo.com/news/amazon-expected-to-report-a-strong-quarter-on-the-backs-of-anthropics-claude-ai-demand-133116973.html", "text": "When Amazon (AMZN) reports earnings on April 29, it may have some bullish numbers to convey to Wall Street.\n\nAnd AI juggernaut Anthropic (ANTH.PVT) may be a large reason why.\n\n\"We believe AWS [Amazon Web Services] is benefiting from a combination of capacity gains, AI diffusion, and client expansion,\" KeyBanc analyst Justin Patterson said in a note on Monday. \"Anthropic has been a long-standing AWS customer, and its rapid growth in annual recurring revenue (from $9 billion in December 2025 to $30 billion in early April 2026) provides a meaningful tailwind to AWS growth (we assume AWS is about 60% of Anthropic spend).\"\n\nAnthropic has had a huge year on the development front, likely pointing to the need to extract as much AWS cloud computing power as possible.\n\nThis month, the company released Claude Opus 4.7 \u2014 its most advanced reasoning model to date. It's the third-highest valued private company, according to Yahoo Finance data. It has reportedly received investor interest at a $800 billion valuation recently.\n\nBesides Anthropic, KeyBanc's Patterson said Amazon AWS will benefit from strong AI-related demand. It's a view supported by strong earnings results out of chip manufacturer Taiwan Semiconductor (TSM) last week.\n\nIncreasing adoption of AI \"increases the likelihood of 30% year over year AWS growth in the first quarter, with further acceleration likely in 2026,\" Patterson said.\n\n\"Finally, we note Amazon CEO Andy Jassy seemed open-minded in the annual shareholder letter to selling Trainium chips to third-parties,\" Patterson added. \"Chips have already surpassed $20B in revenue (>triple-digit year over year growth) through AWS, implying there could be one more growth lever to pull.\""}
